id="top"></a>\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n\t\t\t<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">List of retired Atlantic hurricane names</h1>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">\n\t\t\t\t\tJump to:\t\t\t\t\t<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, \t\t\t\t\t<a href="#p-search">search</a>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:377px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Retired_Atlantic_hurricane_tracks.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Retired_Atlantic_hurricane_tracks.png/375px-Retired_Atlantic_hurricane_tracks.png" width="375" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Retired_Atlantic_hurricane_tracks.png/563px-Retired_Atlantic_hurricane_tracks.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Retired_Atlantic_hurricane_tracks.png/750px-Retired_Atlantic_hurricane_tracks.png 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="2472" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Retired_Atlantic_hurricane_tracks.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nCumulative tracks of retired Atlantic hurricanes until 2012</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>This is a cumulative list of previously used <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclone</a> (tropical storm and hurricane) names which have been indefinitely removed from reuse in the North Atlantic region.</p>\n<p>The naming of North Atlantic tropical cyclones is currently under the oversight of the Hurricane Committee of the <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a>. This group maintains six alphabetic lists of names, with one list used each year. This normally results in each name being reused every six years. However, in the case of a particularly deadly or damaging storm, that storm\'s name is retired, and a replacement starting with the same letter is selected to take its place. The decision whether to remove a name is made yearly at an annual session of the Hurricane Committee.</p>\n<p>The practice of retiring storm names was begun by the United States Weather Bureau in 1955, after major hurricanes <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Carol" title="Hurricane Carol">Carol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Edna" title="Hurricane Edna">Edna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Hazel" title="Hurricane Hazel">Hazel</a> struck the Northeastern United States during the previous year. Initially their names were retired for 10 years, after which time they could be reintroduced; however, in 1969, the policy was changed to have the names retired indefinitely. In 1977, the United States <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a> (NOAA) transferred control of the naming lists to the Hurricane Committee.</p>\n<p>Since the formal start of naming during the <a href="/wiki/1947_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="1947 Atlantic hurricane season">1947 Atlantic hurricane season</a>, an average of one Atlantic storm name has been retired each year, though many seasons (most recently <a href="/wiki/2014_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2014 Atlantic hurricane season">2014</a>) did not have any names retired. The deadliest storm to have its name retired was <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Mitch" title="Hurricane Mitch">Hurricane Mitch</a>, which caused over 10,000 fatalities when it struck Central America during October 1998, while the costliest storm was <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a>, which caused over $108 billion in damage when it struck the U.S. Gulf Coast in August 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-Damage_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Damage-1">[nb 1]</a></sup> The most recently retired storm name is <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Otto" title="Hurricane Otto">Hurricane Otto</a>, which struck <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Costa_Rica" title="Costa Rica">Costa Rica</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nicaragua" title="Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> in late-November 2016.</p>\n<p></p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc">\n<div class="toctitle">\n<h2>Contents</h2>\n</div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Background"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Background</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Names_retired_in_the_1950s"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Names retired in the 1950s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#Names_retired_in_the_1960s"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Names retired in the 1960s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Names_retired_in_the_1970s"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Names retired in the 1970s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#Names_retired_in_the_1980s"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Names retired in the 1980s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-6"><a href="#Names_retired_in_the_1990s"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Names retired in the 1990s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-7"><a href="#Names_retired_in_the_2000s"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Names retired in the 2000s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a href="#Names_retired_in_the_2010s"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Names retired in the 2010s</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">11.1</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">11.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_Atlantic_hurricane_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_tropical_cyclone_naming" title="History of tropical cyclone naming">History of tropical cyclone naming</a></div>\n<p>By 1947, tropical cyclones developing in the North Atlantic Ocean were named by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army_Air_Forces" title="United States Army Air Forces">United States Army Air Forces</a> in private communications between weather centres and aircraft using the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Army/Navy_Phonetic_Alphabet" title="Joint Army/Navy Phonetic Alphabet">Phonetic alphabet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-2">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SRH_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SRH-3">[2]</a></sup> This practice continued until September 1950, when the names started to be used publicly after three hurricanes (<a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Baker_(1950)" title="Hurricane Baker (1950)">Baker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Dog_(1950)" title="Hurricane Dog (1950)">Dog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Easy_(1950)" title="Hurricane Easy (1950)">Easy</a>) had occurred simultaneously and caused confusion within the media and the public.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-2">[1]</a></sup> Over the next 2 years the public use of the phonetic alphabet to name systems continued before at the 1953 Interdepartmental Hurricane Conference it was decided to start using a new list of female names during that season, as a second phonetic alphabet had been developed.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-2">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GP_July_07_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GP_July_07-5">[4]</a></sup> During the active but mild <a href="/wiki/1953_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="1953 Atlantic hurricane season">1953 Atlantic hurricane season</a>, the names were readily used in the press with few objections recorded; as a result, the same names were reused during the next year with only one change: Gilda for Gail. Over the next 6 years a new list of names was developed ahead of each season, before in 1960 forecasters developed four alphabetical sets and repeated them every four years.<sup id="cite_ref-GP_August_07_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GP_August_07-6">[5]</a></sup> These new sets followed the example of the typhoon names and excluded names beginning with the letters Q, U, X, Y, and Z, and keeping them to female names only.<sup id="cite_ref-GP_August_07_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GP_August_07-6">[5]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 1955, it was decided to start retiring the names of significant tropical cyclones for 10 years after which they might be reintroduced, with the names Carol and Edna reintroduced ahead of the 1965 and 1968 hurricane seasons respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-2">[1]</a></sup> At the 1969 Interdepartmental hurricane conference the naming lists were revised after it was decided that the names Carol, Edna and Hazel, would be permanently retired because of their importance to the research community.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-2">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Anna_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anna-7">[6]</a></sup> It was also decided that any significant hurricane in the future would also be permanently retired.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-2">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Anna_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anna-7">[6]</a></sup> Ahead of the 1971 Atlantic hurricane season, 10 lists of hurricane names were inaugurated, by the <a href="/wiki/National_Oceanic_and_Atmospheric_Administration" title="National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration">National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration</a>. In 1977 it was decided that the <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a>\'s Hurricane Committee (WMO) would control the names used, who subsequently decided that six lists of names would be used in the Atlantic Ocean from 1979 onwards with male names included.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-2">[1]</a></sup> Since 1979 the same six lists have been used by the United States <a href="/wiki/National_Hurricane_Center" title="National Hurricane Center">National Hurricane Center</a> <small>(NHC)</small> to name systems, with names of significant tropical cyclones retired from the lists permanently and replaced with new names as required at the following year\'s hurricane committee meeting.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-2">[1]</a></sup></p>\n<p>At present, the name of any tropical cyclone may be retired or withdrawn from the list of names at the request of a member state, if it acquires notoriety for various reasons including the number of deaths, amount of damages or other impacts.<sup id="cite_ref-WMO_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WMO-8">[7]</a></sup> The committee subsequently discuss the proposal and either through building consensus or a majority vote decides if the name should be retired or withdrawn. In March&#160;2017, members of the British Caribbean Territories proposed that a third retirement criterion be added: the tropical cyclone must have sustained winds of at least 96&#160;mph (154&#160;km/h). This came in light of the retirement of <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Erika" title="Tropical Storm Erika">Tropical Storm Erika</a> in 2015 which caused catastrophic flooding and mudslides in Dominica without producing sustained tropical storm-force winds on the island. No action has been taken on this proposal yet.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names_retired_in_the_1950s">Names retired in the 1950s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_Atlantic_hurricane_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Names retired in the 1950s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Between 1954 and 1959, eight names were deemed significant enough to be retired for 10 years due to their impact, before being permanently retired after 1969. There were no names retired after the 1956, 1958, and 1959 seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-WMO_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WMO-8">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HRD_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HRD-10">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NHC_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHC-11">[10]</a></sup> Collectively, these storms resulted in at least 2090 fatalities and over <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7009205500000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>$2.06&#160;billion in damage. The deadliest hurricane was Hurricane Hazel, which killed at least 701 people, while the costliest was Hurricane Diane, which caused <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7008856000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>US$856&#160;million in damage.</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" width="100%">\n<tr>\n<th scope="col" width="6%">Name</th>\n<th scope="col" id="Names_retired_in_the_1960s">Names retired in the 1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_Atlantic_hurricane_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Names retired in the 1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Betsy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Hurricane_Betsy.jpg/220px-Hurricane_Betsy.jpg" width="220" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Hurricane_Betsy.jpg/330px-Hurricane_Betsy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Hurricane_Betsy.jpg/440px-Hurricane_Betsy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1196" data-file-height="1152" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Betsy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Betsy" title="Hurricane Betsy">Hurricane Betsy</a> was the first hurricane to have damages exceeding US$1&#160;billion.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In 1960, four rotating lists of names were developed to avoid having to create new lists each year, while the practice of retiring any particularly damaging storm names for 10&#160;years continued, with 11 names deemed significant enough to be retired during the decade.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-2">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MWLMAR60_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MWLMAR60-25">[22]</a></sup> At the 1969 Hurricane Warning Conference, the National Hurricane Center requested that Carol, Edna, Hazel, and Inez be permanently retired due to their importance to the research community.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-2">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup> This request was subsequently accepted and led to today\'s practice of retiring names of significant tropical cyclones permanently.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_2-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-2">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Anna_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anna-7">[6]</a></sup> There were no names retired after the 1962 and the 1968 seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-TCOP_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TCOP-22">[nb 2]</a></sup> Collectively, the 11 systems were responsible for over 9000 fatalities and in excess of <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7009441000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>US$4.41&#160;billion in damage.</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" width="100%">\n<tr>\n<th scope="col" width="6%">Name</th>\n<th scope="col" class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_Atlantic_hurricane_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Names retired in the 1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:David_30_aug_1979_1906Z.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/David_30_aug_1979_1906Z.jpg/220px-David_30_aug_1979_1906Z.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/David_30_aug_1979_1906Z.jpg/330px-David_30_aug_1979_1906Z.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/David_30_aug_1979_1906Z.jpg/440px-David_30_aug_1979_1906Z.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="640" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:David_30_aug_1979_1906Z.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nHurricane David at its peak intensity.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Starting in 1979, the <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a> began assigning both male and female names to tropical cyclones.<sup id="cite_ref-Mahina_2-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mahina-2">[1]</a></sup> This decade featured hurricanes <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_David" title="Hurricane David">David</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Frederic" title="Hurricane Frederic">Frederic</a>, the first male Atlantic hurricane names to be retired. During this decade, 9 storms were deemed significant enough to have their names retired. Together these 9 systems caused at least <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7009940800000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>$9.41&#160;billion in damage, while more than 10,500 people lost their lives. No names were retired by the Hurricane Committee after the 1971, 1973, or 1976 seasons.</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" width="100%">\n<tr>\n<th scope="col" id="cite_ref-TCOP_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TCOP-22">[nb 2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Intensity_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intensity-24">[nb class="sortkey">7009940800000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>$9.41&#160;billion</th>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names_retired_in_the_1980s">Names retired in the 1980s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_Atlantic_hurricane_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Names retired in the 1980s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Gilbert.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hurricane_Gilbert.jpg/220px-Hurricane_Gilbert.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hurricane_Gilbert.jpg/330px-Hurricane_Gilbert.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hurricane_Gilbert.jpg/440px-Hurricane_Gilbert.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1152" data-file-height="721" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Gilbert.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nHurricane Gilbert at its peak intensity.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>After control of the naming scheme was turned over to the <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">World Meteorological Organization</a>\'s Hurricane Committee during the mid-1970s, the 1980s marked the least prolific decade in terms of the number of retired storms with 7 names warranting removal. Between them the 7 systems caused over <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7010208876000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>$20.9&#160;billion in damage while over 893 people lost their lives. <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Gilbert" title="Hurricane Gilbert">Hurricane Gilbert</a> was the most intense tropical cyclone during the decade by pressure, with a minimum value of 888&#160;hPa (26.22&#160;inHg). This was the lowest recorded pressure in a North Atlantic hurricane until Hurricane Wilma surpassed it during 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-2005_AHS_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2005_AHS-49">[45]</a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Allen" title="Hurricane Allen">Hurricane Allen</a> was the most intense tropical cyclone during the decade by wind speed, with <a href="/wiki/Maximum_sustained_wind" title="Maximum sustained wind">maximum 1\xe2\x80\x93minute sustained winds</a> of 190&#160;mph (305&#160;km/h). This remains the highest sustained wind speed of any Atlantic hurricane on record.<sup id="cite_ref-USA_Today_-_Most_intense_hurricanes_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USA_Today_-_Most_intense_hurricanes-50">[46]</a></sup> No names were retired by the Hurricane Committee after the 1981, 1982, 1984, 1986, or 1987 seasons, which was the most of any decade since the introduction of the practice of retiring hurricane names.</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" width="100%">\n<tr>\n<th scope="col" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intensity-24">[nb 3]</a></sup></th>\n<th>893</th>\n<th><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7010208876000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>$20.9&#160;billion</th>\n<th></th>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names_retired_in_the_1990s">Names retired in the 1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_Atlantic_hurricane_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Names retired in the 1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg/220px-Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg/330px-Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg/440px-Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1832" data-file-height="1200" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Destruction_following_hurricane_andrew.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nDamage after Hurricane Andrew in Miami.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>During the 1990s, the Atlantic Ocean moved into its active era, which led to more tropical cyclones forming during the hurricane seasons. The decade featured <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Andrew" title="Hurricane Andrew">Hurricane Andrew</a> which at the time was the costliest hurricane on record, and also <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Mitch" title="Hurricane Mitch">Hurricane Mitch</a> which is considered to be the deadliest tropical cyclone to have its name retired killing over 11,000 people in Central America. A total of 15 names were retired in this decade, with seven of those during the 1995 and 1996 seasons. There were no names retired after the 1993, 1994 and 1997 seasons.</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" width="100%">\n<tr>\n<th scope="col" colspan="5">References:<sup id="cite_ref-TCOP_22-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TCOP-22">[nb 2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Intensity_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Intensity-24">[nb 3]</a></sup></th>\n<th><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7004121440000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>12,144</th>\n<th><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7010620455604950000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>$62&#160;billion</th>\n<th></th>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names_retired_in_the_2000s">Names retired in the 2000s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_Atlantic_hurricane_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Names retired in the 2000s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Charley_Landfall.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Charley_Landfall.gif/200px-Charley_Landfall.gif" width="200" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Charley_Landfall.gif/300px-Charley_Landfall.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ca/Charley_Landfall.gif 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="270" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Charley_Landfall.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Charley" title="Hurricane Charley">Hurricane Charley</a> making landfall on August 13, 2004 at its peak intensity.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>After the Atlantic basin had moved into the warm phase of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_multidecadal_oscillation" title="Atlantic multidecadal oscillation">Atlantic multidecadal oscillation</a> during the mid-1990s, the 2000s marked the most prolific decade in terms of the number of retired storms, with 24 names warranting removal.<sup id="cite_ref-TCOP_22-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TCOP-22">[nb 2]</a></sup> The decade featured one of the costliest tropical cyclones on record, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a>, which inflicted roughly <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7011108000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>US$108&#160;billion in damage across the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="Gulf Coast of the United States">Gulf Coast of the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-costliest_33-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-costliest-33">[30]</a></sup> Katrina was also the deadliest hurricane to strike the United States since the <a href="/wiki/1928_Okeechobee_hurricane" title="1928 Okeechobee hurricane">1928 Okeechobee hurricane</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-US_Deadliest_12-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-US_Deadliest-12">[11]</a></sup> After causing approximately <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7009900000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>US$9&#160;billion in damage, <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Allison" title="Tropical Storm Allison">Tropical Storm Allison</a> became the first tropical storm in this basin to have its name retired, while subtropical storms started to be named during 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ATL_Book_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ATL_Book-87">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Jeanne" title="Hurricane Jeanne">Hurricane Jeanne</a> was the deadliest storm during the decade and was responsible for over 3000 deaths, when it impacted Haiti and other parts of the Caribbean as a tropical storm and minimal hurricane.<sup id="cite_ref-Jeanne_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jeanne-88">[84]</a></sup> During October 2005, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Wilma" title="Hurricane Wilma">Hurricane Wilma</a> became the most intense tropical cyclone in the Atlantic basin on record, with a central pressure of 882&#160;hPa (26.05&#160;inHg).<sup id="cite_ref-2005_AHS_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2005_AHS-49">[45]</a></sup> There were no retired names after the 2006 and 2009 hurricane seasons.<sup id="cite_ref-NHC_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHC-11">[10]</a></sup> Collectively, the 24 systems were responsible for nearly 7,900 fatalities and in excess of <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7011280247700000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>US$280&#160;billion in damage.</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" class="sortkey">7011280247700000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>$280&#160;billion</th>\n<th></th>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names_retired_in_the_2010s">Names retired in the 2010s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_retired_Atlantic_hurricane_names&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Names retired in the 2010s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sandy_Oct_29_2012_1540Z.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Sandy_Oct_29_2012_1540Z.png/220px-Sandy_Oct_29_2012_1540Z.png" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Sandy_Oct_29_2012_1540Z.png/330px-Sandy_Oct_29_2012_1540Z.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Sandy_Oct_29_2012_1540Z.png/440px-Sandy_Oct_29_2012_1540Z.png 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1080" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sandy_Oct_29_2012_1540Z.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nHurricane Sandy hours before landfall on October 29, 2012.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>So far during the current decade, nine tropical cyclone names have been retired.<sup id="cite_ref-TCOP_22-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TCOP-22">[nb 2]</a></sup> Collectively, these systems killed at least 1117 people and caused at least <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7011109537350000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>$110&#160;billion worth of damage. So far, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Igor" title="Hurricane Igor">Hurricane Igor</a> is the most intense tropical cyclone during the decade whose name has been retired by pressure, with a minimum value of 924&#160;hPa (27.29&#160;inHg). In addition, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Matthew" title="Hurricane Matthew">Hurricane Matthew</a> is the deadliest and most intense tropical cyclone by wind speed during the decade, with over 600 deaths and <a href="/wiki/Maximum_sustained_wind" title="Maximum sustained wind">maximum 1\xe2\x80\x93minute sustained winds</a> of 165&#160;mph (270&#160;km/h), respectively. <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Sandy" title="Hurricane Sandy">Hurricane Sandy</a> is currently the costliest Atlantic hurricane in the 2010s as well as the second-costliest in history, with <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7010750000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>US$75&#160;billion in damage attributed to it. 2014 saw no retired names.<sup id="cite_ref-NHC_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHC-11">[10]</a></sup></p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<table class="wikitable sortable" width="100%">\n<tr>\n<th scope="col" width="5%">Name</th>\n<th scope="col"
